B i l a n i n , M i l t o n E. Teske,\nColeman duP. Donaldson and R i c h a r d S . Snedeker\nAeronautical Research Associates o f Princeton, I n c .\nSUMMARY\n\nThe mechanism o f merging o f l i k e - s i g n e d a i r c r a f t v o r t i c e s leads\n\nt o a r a p i d r e d i s t r i b u t i o n o f t r a i l e d v o r t i c i t y i n a wake t h r o u g h\nb o t h c o n v e c t i v e and t u r b u l e n t p r o c e s s e s . Merging i s i n v e s t i g a t e d\ne x p e r i m e n t a l l y i n a small wind t u n n e l and a n a l y t i c a l l y t h r o u g h t h e\nu s e o f a code which computes t u r b u l e n t t r a n s p o r t u s i n g a secondo r d e r c l o s u r e t u r b u l e n t model. Computations a r e r e p o r t e d which\nd e m o n s t r a t e t h e merging phenomenon,and comparisons a r e made w i t h\ne x p e r i m e n t a l r e s u l t s . The u s e f u l n e s s o f p o i n t v o r t e x c o m p u t a t i o n s\ni n p r e d i c t i n g merging i s e x p l o r e d . L i m i t e d c o m p u t a t i o n s have shown\nt h a t j e t e x h a u s t does n o t a p p r e c i a b l y a l t e r t h e merging phenomenon.\nThe e f f e c t o f ambient a t m o s p h e r i c t u r b u l e n c e on t h e a g i n g o f an\na i r c r a f t wake i s i n v e s t i g a t e d a t c o n s t a n t t u r b u l e n t d i s s i p a t i o n\nr a t e . It i s shown t h a t under s t a b l e a t m o s p h e r i c c o n d i t i o n s , when\na t m o s p h e r i c m a c r o s c a l e s a r e l e s s t h a n or e q u a l t o t h e v o r t e x\ns p a c i n g , m i s l e a d i n g r e s u l t s may b e o b t a i n e d . T h i s c o n c l u s i o n\nc a u t i o n s a g a i n s t u s i n g one p a r a m e t e r t o c h a r a c t e r i z e t h e a b i l i t y o f\nt h e atmosphere t o d i s s i p a t e a i r c r a f t wake v o r t i c e s .\n\n8\n\nThe work r e p o r t e d here has been s p o n s o r e d by NASA under C o n t r a c t\nNo. NAS1-13932 and by t h e AFOSR under C o n t r a c t No. F44620-75-C0051.\nThe a u t h o r s would l i k e t o t h a n k M r . A QUALITATIVE DESCRIPTION OF VORTEX WAKE A G I N G\nWhen a n a i r c r a f t i n a c l e a n ( c r u i s e ) c o n f i g u r a t i o n t r a i l s a\nwake, two r e g i o n s o f somewhat c o n c e n t r a t e d v o r t i c i t y are l e f t i n\nt h e f l u i d , shown s c h e m a t i c a l l y i n f i g u r e 3. F o r a n e n c o u n t e r i n g\na i r c r a f t whose span i s l e s s t h a n t h e semispan of t h e g e n e r a t i n g\na i r c r a f t , a most d e s i r a b l e p i e c e o f i n f o r m a t i o n i s t h e i n t e n s i t y\nof t h e s w i r l i n g f l o w i n a c i r c u l a r area whose diameter i s t h e\nwingspan o f t h e e n c o u n t e r i n g a i r c r a f t . I f c i r c u l a t i o n i s a\n65\n\nr e a s o n a b l e measure o f t h i s i n t e n s i t y , t h e n w e must d e t e r m i n e how\nc i r c u l a t i o n might d e c r e a s e i n t h i s r e g i o n , I f w e c e n t e r t h e area\nabout t h e c e n t r o i d o f shed v o r t i c i t y ( a s i n f i g . 3 ) , w e see t h a t\nv o r t i c i t y c a n n o t b e t r a n s p o r t e d from t h i s r e g i o n by c o n v e c t i o n\ns i n c e , r o u g h l y s p e a k i n g , s t r e a m l i n e s do n o t l e a v e t h e c i r c u l a r\nr e g i o n . Hence, d i f f u s i o n i s t h e o n l y mechanism able t o spread\nv o r t i c i t y and r e d u c e c i p c u l a t i o n i n t h i s a r e a . U n f o r t u n a t e l y ,\nt h e a x i s y m m e t r i c v o r t e x i s q u i t e s t a b l e and can o n l y s u s t a i n low\nt u r b u l e n c e l e v e l s . I n f a c t , t u r b u l e n c e i s a c t u a l l y damped i n t h e\nc e n t r a l r e g i o n o f t h e v o r t e x ( t h e v i s c o u s c o r e ) . It i s j u s t i n\nt h i s r e g i o n where v o r t i c i t y i s maximum, making i t d e s i r a b l e to d i s t r i b u t e t h i s v o r t i c i t y r a t h e r e x t e n s i v e l y o v e r t h e wake h a l f - p l a n e .\nA p p a r e n t l y , however, o n l y m o l e c u l a r t r a n s p o r t p r o v i d e s any d i f f u s i o n\nof v o r t i c i t y h e r e .\nThat v o r t e x c o r e s from s i m p l y l o a d e d wings a r e i n f a c t n e a r l y\nl a m i n a r c a n b e s e e n from f i g u r e 4 where smoke r e l e a s e d from a t o w e r\nhas been e n t r a i n e d i n t o t h e wing t i p v o r t e x o f a l a r g e a i r c r a f t .\nThe a i r t h r o u g h which t h e a i r c r a f t f l i e s i s d i s c e r n i b l y t u r b u l e n t\n( a s i n t e r p r e t e d from t h e d i s p e r s i o n of smoke) w h i l e t h e v o r t i c a l\nf l u i d making up t h e v i s c o u s c o r e i s n e a r l y l a m i n a r .\n\nT u r b u l e n t c o m p u t a t i o n s u s i n g second- order c l o s u r e modeling\na l s o c o n f i r m t h i s f e a t u r e of i s o l a t e d v o r t i c e s . The s w i r l i n g\nv e l o c i t y f o r a c l a s s i c a l Lamb v o r t e x i s g i v e n by\n\nTaking t h e e q u i l i b r i u m , n o n d i f f u s i v e , and h i g h Reynolds number\nl i m i t s o f t h e second- order c l o s u r e model ( r e f . 6 ) , w e o b t a i n t h e\nt u r b u l e n t k i n e t i c e n e r g y d i s t r i b u t i o n shown i n f i g u r e 5 . I f t h e\nmerging phenomenon c a n b e u n d e r s t o o d and p r e d i c t e d , a great pot e n t i a l e x i s t s f o r l e s s e n i n g the hazard a s s o c i a t e d w i t h vortex\nwakes t h r o u g h s u i t a b l y t a i l o r e d wing l i f t and d r a g d i s t r i b u t i o n s .\n\n67\n\n1 1\n1 .\n\nA NUMERICAL COMPUTATION OF MERGING\n\nDunham ( r e f . 9 ) f i r s t o b s e r v e d t h e merging phenomenon between\nf l a p and t i p v o r t i c e s o f t h e B- 747 a i r c r a f t on what has become t h e\nLDG/O c o n f i g u r a t i o n . The r a t h e r r a p i d d i f f u s i o n\no f t h e i n k s u g g e s t e d t h e mechanism o f t u r b u l e n t t r a n s p o r t i n t h e\nspreading of the t r a i l e d v o r t i c i t y .\nTo model t h e c o n v e c t i o n and t u r b u l e n t d i f f u s i o n o f v o r t i c i t y ,\nwe a t A . R . A . P . have d e v e l o p e d a computer code to s o l v e t h e\ne q u a t i o n s o f f l u i d motion i n c l u d i n g o u r i n v a r i a n t t u r b u l e n t model\n( r e f . 10). T h i s model i s based on a c l o s u r e o f t h e r a t e e q u a t i o n s\nf o r t h e v e l o c i t y c o r r e l a t i o n s a t t h e second o r d e r . It i s shown t h a t t h e merging phenomenon between\nl i k e - s i g n e d v o r t i c e s i s a mechanism which r e s u l t s i n r e d i s t r i b u t i o n\no f t h e t r a i l e d v o r t i c i t y t h r o u g h c o n v e c t i v e and t u r b u l e n t mechanisms.\nT h i s r e d i s t r i b u t i o n i s one which e x t e n s i v e l y spreads t h e v o r t i c i t y\no v e r t h e wake and t h e r e f o r e h a s t e n s t h e d e c a y p r o c e s s . Comparisons\nbetween e x p e r i m e n t a l o b s e r v a t i o n s o f v o r t e x merging and n u m e r i c a l\ns i m u l a t i o n o f t h e phenomenon a r e f a v o r a b l e .\nThe u s e f u l n e s s o f d i s c r e t e p o i n t v o r t e x c o m p u t a t i o n s t o i n -\n\nv e s t i g a t e wake geometry and s t r u c t u r e i s d i s c u s s e d , It i s shown\nt h a t small changes i n t h e s t r e n g t h and/or i n i t i a l p o s i t i o n o f t h e\nf u s e l a g e v o r t e x c a n have a p r o f o u n d e f f e c t on t h e s t r u c t u r e o f t h e\nm u l t i p l e - v o r t e x - p a i r wake.\nUsing t h e t r a n s p o r t a t i v e code, c o m p u t a t i o n s have been r u n\nwhich d e m o n s t r a t e merging between l i k e - s i g n e d and s t r e n g t h f l a p\nand t i p v o r t i c e s w i t h and w i t h o u t e n g i n e j e t e x h a u s t . The merging\nphenomenon d o e s n o t appear t o be s i g n i f i c a n t l y a l t e r e d b y t h e j e t\nexhaust.\nThe e f f e c t o f ambient a t m o s p h e r i c t u r b u l e n c e on t h e a g i n g o f\n\na n a i r c r a f t wake i s i n v e s t i g a t e d a t c o n s t a n t t u r b u l e n t d i s s i p a t i o n\nr a t e . It i s shown t h a t under s t a b l e a t m o s p h e r i c c o n d i t i o n s , when\na t m o s p h e r i c m a c r o s c a l e s may b e l e s s t h a n or e q u a l t o t h e v o r t e x\n85\n\ns p a c i n g , m i s l e a d i n g r e s u l t s may b e o b t a i n e d i f v o r t e x a g i n g i s\nc o r r e l a t e d only w i t h t h e t u r b u l e n t d i s s i p a t i o n rate. This r e s u l t\nc a u t i o n s a g a i n s t u s i n g one parameter t o c h a r a c t e r i z e t h e a b i l i t y\no f t h e atmosphere t o d i s s i p a t e a i r c r a f t wake v o r t i c e s .\nThe t e c h n o l o g y now e x i s t s t o i n v e s t i g a t e t h e complex con-\n\nv e c t i v e and d i f f u s i v e p r o c e s s e s i n a i r c r a f t v o r t e x wakes. However,\nt o u s e t h i s t e c h n o l o g y r e q u i r e s a n a c c u r a t e d e s c r i p t i o n o f t h e wing\nl o a d d i s t r i b u t i o n - a q u a n t i t y n o t e a s i l y p r e d i c t e d f o r all b u t\nc r u i s e - c o n f i g u r e d wings. S t i c k l e\nLangley Research Center\n\nand\n\nMark W. Kelly\nA e \'Research Center\nms\n\nSUMMARY\n\nTo determine t h e f e a s i b i l i t y of a l t e r i n g t h e formation and decay of\na i r c r a f t t r a i l i n g v o r t e x e s through aerodynamic means, NASA used t h e test capab i l i t i e s of two wind t u n n e l s and two towing b a s i n s .\n\nThis paper d e s c r i b e s t h e\n\nf a c i l i t i e s , common models, and measurement techniques t h a t w e r e employed i n\nt h e e v a l u a t i o n of v o r t e x minimization concepts.\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nThe i n i t i a l t a s k of t h e NASA Wake Vortex A l l e v i a t i o n Program w a s t o\ne v a l u a t e t h e many devices and concepts t h a t had been proposed t o a l t e r v o r t e x\nformation and decay. F a c i l i t i e s , t h e r e f o r e , w e r e a major concern because t h e\nc h a r a c t e r i s t i c s of t h e v o r t e x system must b e determined from t h e p o i n t of\ng e n e r a t i o n t o p o i n t s f a r downstream, r e p r e s e n t i n g i n scale t h e area of i n t e r e s t\nt o a following a i r c r a f t .\n\n129\n\n*\n\n\'\n\nThe problems, measurement techniques, and s c a l i n g of Reynolds number\nand v i s c o s i t y e f f e c t s were discussed among both Government and i n d u s t r y res e a r c h e r s i n p r e p a r a t i o n f o r t h e e v a l u a t i o n program. (a) Large probe model. (b) Small probe model.\n155\n\nAPPLICATION OF LASER VELOCIMETRY TO AIRCRAFT WAKE-VORTEX MEASUREMENTS\nDonald L. Ciffone and Kenneth L. Orloff\nAmes Research Center\nSUMMARY\nThe theory and use of a laser velocimeter that makes simultaneous measurements of vertical and longitudinal velocities while rapidly scanning a flow\nfield laterally are described, and its direct application to trailing wakevortex research is discussed.\n\nPertinent measurements of aircraft wake-vortex\n\nvelocity distributions obtained in a wind tunnel and water towing tank are\npresented. The utility of the velocimeter to quantitatively assess differences in wake velocity distributions due to wake dissipating devices and span\nloading changes on the wake-generating model is also demonstrated.\nINTRODUCTION\nAn accurate, quantitative definition of the flow field associated with\n\nlift-generated wake vortices behind aircraft is essential if the fluid dynamics of these wakes are to be completely understood. The recent, increasing\nnumber of both ground-based and flight experimental investigations of wakevortex characteristics include flow visualization studies (refs. 1-8), measurements of induced rolling moments on following aircraft (refs. 5 and 9-12), and\nwake velocity measurements (refs. 13-28).\n\nFlow visualization allows the inves-\n\ntigator to view the structure of the wake, but leaves him to deduce the quantitative results of the fluid dynamic phenomena observed. Following-aircraft\nrolling moment measurements allow the investigator to compare wake alleviation\nconcepts, but once again the basic fluid-dynamic phenomena are "explained"\ndeductively.\n\nOnly a direct measurement of the wake-velocity distributions\n\nprovides a quantitative understanding of the flow field.\n\nThese measurements\n\nhave been attempted using pressure probes (refs. 15 and 16), hot-wire anemometry (refs. 13, 14, 17, 18, and 27), and, more recently, with laser velocimetry\n8.\n(refs. 19-26 and 2 )\n\nThe velocities obtained from a fixed hot wire or\n157\n\npressure probe are subject to doubt because the probe may interfere with the\nstructure of the vortex. Itis questionable whether any mechanical probe can be\nplaced near a small trailing vortex without significantly disturbing the flow.\n\nIn addition, the meandering motion of vortices in test facilities complicates\nmeasurements using stationary probes (refs. 13-17).\n\nThis vortex motion results\n\nin time averaging of velocity measured by a stationary probe, and a consequent l o s s of measurable detailed structure. The use of a high-speed rotating\nhot wire (ref. 18) represents one attempt to circumvent this vortex meander\nproblem.\n\nFlight measurements have been performed using hot-wire anemometry in\n\nwhich an instrumented probe aircraft follows a generator aircraft to measure\nwake velocities.\n\nThese measurements, however, are difficult to interpret and\n\nexpensive to implement. The laser velocimetry measurement technique does not\ndisturb the flow and has high spatial resolution.\n\nIn addition, the processed\n\nsignal from a laser velocimeter is linearly related to the velocity sensed and\nrequires no calibration. The result is a quantitative definition cf the flow\nThese properties of the laser velocimeter\n\nfield which is easy to interpret.\n\nmake it ideal for studying the fluid dynamics of aircraft trailing wakes.\nA laser velocimeter developed at Ames Research Center, which makes simultaneous measurements of vertical and longitudinal velocities while rapidly\nscanning a flow field laterally, is discussed here.\n\nThis instrument was\n\ndeveloped specifically for wake-vortex measurements. The basic principles of\nlaser velocimetry are reviewed briefly, and some of the more significant\nresults from the application of this instrument to the measurement\nwake-vortex characteristics is presented.\nNOMENCLATURE\nAR\n\nwing aspect ratio\n\nb\n\nwingspan,\n\nC\n\nwing reference chord, m\n\nn?\n\ncL\n\nlift coefficient\n\nr\n\nvortex radius, m\n\nun\nr\n\nfree-stream velocity, m/sec\n158\ni\n\nof\n\naircraft\n\nstreamwise velocity, m/sec\nrotational velocity, m/sec\nmaximum rotational velocity, m/sec\nmaximum rotational velocity in plat\n\nregion, m/sec\n\ndownstream distance from trailing edge of wing, m\nangle of attack, deg\nindex of refraction\ncenterline circulation, m/e\n\'sc\nSubscripts\n\nB\n\nend of plateau region\n\nP\n\nplateau region\nLASER VELOCIMETRY TECHNIQUES\nNot long after the introduction of the gas laser in the early 1 9 6 0 \' ~ ~\n\nscientists discovered a way to use the laser to measure the velocity of small\nmicron-sized particles entrained within a gas or liquid. These particles are\nassumed to move along the streamlines of the flow.\n\nIf the drag forces acting\n\non the particles dominate their inertia forces, then a measurement of their\nvelocity represents an accurate measurement of the fluid velocity at the location of the particle.\n\nThis technique has become known as "laser velocimetry,"\n\nand instruments designed to accomplish this measurement are called "laser\nvelocimeters.I1\n\nThe "dual-scatter" or "fringe" mode laser velocimeter has\n\ngained widespread acceptance as a result of the ease with which it can be\noptically aligned and the relatively high signal/noise ratios realized in the\nmeasurement of the velocity of particles.\n\nIn this type of velocimeter, the\n\noutput beam of a laser is passed through a prism dividing it into two parallel,\ncoherent, beams of equal light intensity, which are then focused through a\nconvex lens to intersect at a point in the flow field where the velocity is to\nbe measured.\n\nThe velocimeter signal is generated from within the ellipsoidal\n159\n\nvolume c r e a t e d by t h i s i n t e r s e c t i o n of t h e two l i g h t beams.\n\nThis so- called\n\nprobe volume varies i n atmospheric\nresearch.\n\nBecause of t h e a n g l e o f i n t e r s e c t i o n of t h e l i g h t beams, a p a t t e r n\n\nof e v e n l y spaced, a l t e r n a t e l y l i g h t and d a r k i n t e the wake-generating model and\n0.\nilluminating them with a system of lights (fig. 1 )\n\nA separate, high-speed\n\nscan - short focal length - laser system with a high scan sensitivity is being\ndeveloped for use with this flow visualization system to quantitatively assess\nthe flow fields o f multiple vortex wakes.\nVORTEX-VELOCITY MEASUREMENTS\nThe scanning, two-color, dual-beam, backscatter; argon-ion laser velocimeter described previously has been used to measure the vertical and streamwise\nvelocity components in wakes containing vortices.\n\nThe measurements have been\n\nmade both in a wind tunnel (refs. 12-21, 24, and 31) and a water-towing\nfacility (refs. 22, 23, 25, and 2 )\n6.\n\nThe need for a rapid scanning instrument\n\ndiffered for the two facilities. For the wind tunnel, the trailing vortices\nare fully established but may wander in a random manner.\n\nThe center of the\n\nvortex does not remain stationary within the test section because large-scale\neddies are convected through the wind tunnel.\n\nHence the vortex must be tra-\n\nversed during a time which is short compared to the movement of the vortex.\nA s discussed previously, the optical scanning is performed in a direction nor-\n\nm a l to the tunnel centerline.\n\nThis is shown schematically in figure 11 for a\n\nsemispan model and photographically in figure 12 for a 1.83-m (6-ft) span\nmodel of the Boeing 747. The model was mounted in an inverted position to\nminimize the interference of the support strut with the wake.\nFor the towing tank, rapid scanning at a given measurement station is\nrequired because of the time-dependent nature of the trailing vortices as the\nwing model is towed through the tank.\n\nThe flow field does not have perturba-\n\ntions of the vortex velocity about a mean steady-state velocity, but rather a\ndecaying shear f l o w .\n\nIn this case, a meaningful time history of the vortex\n\nrequires that the time for an optical traversal be short compared with vortex\ndecay time, so that a mean "age" can be assigned to each measured profile. A s\nthe vortex pair moves in the tank due to its mutual influence, it is necessary\nthat the velocimeter moves vertically while scanning to keep the optical axis\nalined with the vortex centerline. Spanwise continuous traversals of the\n\n166\n\nprobe volume through the near-side wake are made with the automatic scan\nreversal set to encompass the area of interest (figs. 13 and 1 )\n4.\nWind-Tunnel Test Results\nThe first application of the laser velocimeter to measure the streamwise\nand rotational velocity distributions in vortices was to a square-tipped,\n0.457-m chord, 1.218-m semispan wing mounted in the Ames 7- by 10-Foot Wind\nTunnel (ref. 2 )\n0.\n\nThe airfoil section was an NACA 0015 and the planform was\n\nrectangular with no twist. Detailed velocity profiles were obtained at a\nscanning rate of 15 cm/sec (fig. 7) and were found to be in general agreement\nwith data (ref. 18) obtained with a hot-wire probe mounted on the end of a\nrapidly rotating boom.\n\nThis velocity information provides for a comparative\n\nevaluation of vortex alleviation schemes, as was demonstrated for a dissipator\npanel mounted on the rectangular wing.\n\nA square panel (4 percent of the\n\nsemispan, 11 percent of the wing chord) was installed at the wing tip at the\n1/4-chord position.\n\nA comparison of the measured velocity distributions\n\nshowed a reduction in maximum rotational velocity and the development of a\nlarge streamwise velocity defect due to the panel.\n\n, as a function of\nV9max\ndownstream distance is summarized in figure 15, together with the results\nThe reduction in maximum rotational velocity,\n\nobtained from the rapidly moving hot-wire data (ref. 18) of a geometrically\nsimilar configuration in the Ames 40- by 80-Foot Wind Tunnel at 55 chord\nlengths downstream.\n\nwas assumed to be the averagt maximum of the two\n(vemax\npeaks of the rotational velocity distribution (fig. 7.\n))\nFrom these results,\nit appears that the mechanism that alleviates the vortex develops with increasing downstream distance.\nThe need for experimental velocity distributions in the wakes of wings\nmore representative of present-day transport aircraft was the rationale for\nthe second series of wind-tunnel tests (ref. 2 )\n1.\n\nVelocity profiles were\n\nobtained in the wake of a Convair-990 semispan wing (fig. 11).\n\nThe effects of\n\nconfiguration buildup (including flaps, flow-through nacelles, and antishock\nbodies) and wingspan loading changes due to flap deflection and angle of\nattack were investigated. At a lift coefficient of 0 . 7 , there is little difference in\nthe downstream characteristics of the maximum rotational velocities in the\nwake of the clean wing or the landing configuration. The results obtained with\nthe tailored configuration are much like those obtained with the diamond wing\nplanform.\n\nThe maximum wake tangential velocities are less than half those of\n\nthe other configurations, and the plateau region extends to 80 span lengths.\nOnce again, this long plateau is instrumental in reducing the initial large\ndifferences in maximum rotary velocities to insignificant differences in the\nfar wake.\nA comparison of the measured radial distribution of streamwise velocity\nin the core of the vortices behind these swept-wing configurations at 7 5 span\nlengths downstream is presented in figure 26. The results show a streamwise\nvelocity defect toward the model for all configurations.\n\nPlateau v e l o c i t y c o r r e l a t i o n - A correlation function based on the selfsimilar turbulent decay of a line vortex has been developed (ref. 39) and\nutilized to substantiate the validity of using ground-based scale model data\nto predict high Reynolds number flight results.\n\nIt was found that if the\n\ndownstream dependence of vortex maximum rotational velocity is presented as a\nvortex velocity scaling parameter, V,b/ToAR, versus a distance scaling parameter, (X/b) (ro/Umb)(AR)2f (To/v), the scale model and flight data collapse to a\nsingle curve.\n\nPresented in figure 27 are the results obtained when these\n\nscaling parameters are used to plot vortex maximum rotational velocity in the\nplateau region versus a corresponding downstream duration of this region\nobtained from the towing tank data presented previously (ref. 26).\n\nThe line\n\nfaired through the experimental points has a slope of -1, which implies the\nfollowing inverse proportionality between the plateau velocity and extent of\nthe plateau region:\n\n171\n\nwas developed in reference 39 and is a\n\nThe Reynolds number parameter, f(r0/v),\nfunction of vortex Reynolds number, r o / v\nf(rO/v) = 1.\n\n=\n\nFor r o / v 2 3x105,\n\n(oUbR()\nr/,)eb.\n\nHence, for all flight conditions, f(To/v)\n\n= 1\n,\n\nand for most\n\nground-based tests (including the water-tank tests of refs. 22 and 25),\nf(r0/v) is of order 1.\nUse of equation (5) and a -1/2 decay region downstream dependence of vortex maximum rotational velocity allows near-field wake velocity measurements\nin the plateau region (i.e., wind-tunnel data) to be used to estimate subsequent far-field vortex characteristics. The lines through the data in\nfigures 18, 19, and 25 predict the downstream variation of maximum rotational\nvelocity with this approach. The vertical bars indicate the predicted end of\nthe plateau region. The predictions and measurements are seen to be in good\nagreement.\nCONCLUDING REMARKS\nThe theory and use of a laser velocimeter with high-speed spatial scanning\ncapability has been described and its direct application to trailing wake vortex research has been presented.\n\nThe scanning feature of this instrument mini-\n\nmizes the difficulties in velocity measurement due to what has been termed\n11\n\nvortex meander."\n\nAccurate vortex definition is achieved and the data are\n\nrepeatable and consistent with other experimental results.\nMeasurements have been made of changes in wake rotational and streamwise\nvelocity components due to span-loading changes of the wake-generating model.\nThe results identified two characteristic flow regions for the dependence of\nvortex maximum rotational velocity on\' downstream distance.\n\nThe first, a region\n\nof constant velocity, can extend from wake rollup to 80 span lengths downstream\nbehind the generating wing, depending on span loading and angle of attack.\nvaries approximately as\n\'mx\nea\nThis implies that inviscid vortex structure calculations should be\n\nThis is followed by a decay region in plakeau\nregion to downstream duration of this region (ref. 26).\n\n192\n\nFLIGHT-TEST TECHNIQUES FOR WAKE-VORTEX MINIMIZATION STUDIES\nRobert A Jacobsen and M R Barber*\n.\n. .\nAmes Research Center\n\nThe flight-test techniques developed for use in the study of wake turbulence and used recently in flight studies of wake minimization methods are\ndiscussed.\n\nFlow visualization has been developed as a technique for qualita-\n\ntively assessing minimization methods and is required in flight-test procedures for making quantitative measurements.\n\nThe quantitative techniques are\n\nthe measurement of the upset dynamics of an aircraft encountering the wake and\nthe measurement of the wake velocity profiles.\n\nDescriptions of the instrumen-\n\ntation and.the data reduction and correlation methods are given.\nINTRODUCTION\nFlight tests have played an important role in the coordinated research\nprogram to develop vortex-wake alleviation techniques. The contribution of\nflight testing to this program lies in three important areas:\n\nto verify that\n\nthe more flexible and less expensive to operate, ground-based research facilities are suitable for the development of vortex alleviation devices and techniques; to identify, under full-scale conditions, shortcomings of any\nalleviation technique that might not have been evident in the small-scale test\nenvironment; and to assess the operational feasibility of the developed techniques.\n\nThe operational feasibility phase has two facets.\n\nThe first is the\n\ninfluence of the alleviation device on the operation of the generating aircraft, but this subject is outside the scope of this paper.\n\nThe second facet\n\nis to demonstrate the level of alleviation attained in the oDerationa1\nsituation.\nRecent flight experience has emphasized the importance of flight tests in\ncomplementing and focusing the research efforts in the ground-based facilities.\nA s a result of wind-tunnel studies at Ames and Langley Research Centers, it\n\n*Flight Research Center.\n193\n\nwas discovered that raising the outboard flap segment in the landing configuration of a Boeing 747 markedly reduces the roliing moments induced on a following model (ref. 1.\n)\n\nFlight tests confirm that the vortex-induced upset can be\n\nreduced substantially through this flap modification.\n\nHowever, the flight-\n\ntest results show that the alleviation is greatly diminished when the landing\ngear is extended (ref. 2.\n)\n\nThis effect of gear was unexpected and hence had\n\nbeen overlooked in the wind tunnels.\nimportant effects\n\nThe flight test, therefore, had two\n\n- confirming that the wind-tunnel test results were meaning-\n\nful and focusing future research on configurations -that provide alleviation in\nthe presence of a lowered landing gear.\nThree flight-test techniques for wake turbulence research have evolved.\nThe development of these techniques was initiated before the wake-vortex\nalleviation program s o that the hazards associated with the vortex systems\ncould be documented from a number of jet aircraft.\n\nThe three techniques are\n\nflow visualization, upset measurements of encountering aircraft, and velocity\nprofile measurements.\nFlow visualization is useful in comparisons of various alleviation configurations.\n\nHowever, the primary value of flow visualization is to make it\n\nfeasible for the pilot of a probe aircraft to locate the vortex and to penetrate its core so that the other two techniques can be applied.\n\nHistorically,\n\nthe first flow visualization simply relied on the entrainment of the jet\nThis system was marginally successful for the\n\nexhaust in the vortices.\n\ntightly rolled-up vortex systems generated by transport aircraft in the cruise\nconfiguration and, for some aircraft, in the landing configuration.\n\nHowever,\n\nfor most aircraft in the landing configuration, with or without alleviation,\nspecially developed flow visualization techniques must be used.\n\nAs might be\n\nexpected, the more effective an alleviation technique is in eliminating or\nspreading the organized flow within the wake, the more difficult it becomes to\nmark the wake effectively.\nTechniques for evaluating the hazard due to a wake vortex by measuring\nthe upset of a penetrating aircraft were well developed before the alleviation\nprogram was initiated.\n\nEarly efforts included measurements of the wakes of\n\nlarge jet aircraft (such as the C-5A), with a number of smaller aircraft ranging in size from a Convair CV-990 to a Cessna 210 (refs, 3 - 6 ) .\n\n194\n\nResponse\n\nmeasurements were used i n t h e e a r l i e s t f l i g h t - t e s t program t o \' a s s e s s t h e\ne f f e c t of an a l l e v i a t i o n device.\nThis experiment was conducted a t A m e s i n 1970 u s i n g a s p o i l e r on t h e\nwingtip of t h e CV-990 ( r e f . 7).\n\nT h i s d e v i c e had been developed i n t h e wind\nI n f l i g h t , the e f f e c t of t h e\n\nt u n n e l on t h e b a s i s of t u f t - g r i d s t u d i e s .\n\ns p o i l e r w a s from t h e o t h e r .\n\nThe p i l o t s could n o t d i s c e r n any e f f e c t of t h i s\n\ndevice.\n\nHowever, d a t a r e d u c t i o n d i d i n d i c a t e t h a t t h e r e was some a l l e v i a t i o n\n\neffect.\n\nTIie p r i n c i p a l r e s u l t of t h i s f l i g h t program was t o convince r e s e a r c h -\n\ners a t Ames t h a t more d e f i n i t i v e f l i g h t - t e s t e\ncalculations.\nI n t h e following s e c t i o n s , t h e c u r r e n t s t a t e of development of each\nf l i g h t - t e s t technique i s d i s c u s s e d i n g r e a t e r d e t a i l .\nFLIGHT-TEST TECHNIQUES\n\nFlow V i s u a l i z a t i o n\nFlow v i s u a l i z a t i o n has been a primary concern throughout t h e development\nof wake t u r b u l e n c e f l i g h t - t e s t (Long Beach, C a l i f . ) .\n\nF i g u r e 1 shows one of t h e s e d e v i c e s\n\nminum c y l i n d e r approximately 25 c m (10 in.)\nw i t h a 0.7-m (2.2- ft)\n\ntailpipe.\n\n-\n\nan alu-\n\ni n diameter and 1.4 m (4.5 f t ) long\n\nThe "smoke" generated by t h i s device i s\n\na c t u a l l y small d r o p l e t s of m i n e r a l o i l c r e a t e d when o i l vapor e x p e l l e d by t h e\nsmoke g e n e r a t o r i s condensed by t h e c o o l e r ambient a i r .\n\nA combustor contained\n\nw i t h i n t h e c y l i n d e r g e n e r a t e s t h e h e a t used t o v a p o r i z e t h e m i n e r a l o i l .\n\nThe\n\no i l i s sprayed i n t o t h e exhaust of t h e combustor i n t h e t a i l p i p e extending\nbehind t h e c y l i n d e r .\n\nThe f u e l f o r t h e combustor and t h e m i n e r a l o i l are con-\n\ntained i n tanks located within the cylinder.\n\nThe s e l f - c o n t a i n e d tankage pro-\n\nv i d e s f o r 20 min of o p e r a t i o n w i t h ambient a i r being used t o support t h e combustion.\n\nA t t h e approach speeds used f o r much of t h e wake t u r b u l e n c e t e s t i n g ,\n\nt h e o p e r a t i o n of t h e combustor on ambient a i r i s marginal.\n\nThe d e v i c e w a s\n\nmodified r e c e n t l y t o a l l o w engine b l e e d a i r t o b e used i n t h e combustor.\n\nThis\n\nchange g r e a t l y improved t h e "smoke" production, b u t t h e o p e r a t i n g t i m e i s\nreduced t o 1 0 min.\nI n a t y p i c a l i n s t a l l a t i o n on a r e s e a r c h a i r c r a f t , one o r more smoke gene r a t o r s i s mounted under t h e wing a t each spanwise l o c a t i o n where a d i s c r e t e\nv o r t e x i s expected t o form ( f i g . 2).\n\nThese l o c a t i o n s t y p i c a l l y i n c l u d e t h e\n\nw i n g t i p , edges of t h e f l a p segments, and t h e wing/fuselage j u n c t i o n .\n\nThe two\n\nmost r e c e n t i n s t a l l a t i o n s have b o t h been on a B-747 used f o r t h e wake a l l e v i a t i o n program a t F l i g h t Research Center (Edwards A i r Force Base, C a l i f . ) .\nt h e f i r s t of t h e two i n s t a l l a t i o n s ( f i g . 2 ) , a s i n g l e smoke g e n e r a t o r w a s\n\n196\n\nIn\n\nmounted at the tip, one at the outboard edge of the outboard flap segment and\none at the outboard edge of the inboard flap segment of each wing.\n\nThe flow\n\nvisualization provided by this installation was often marginal and improvements were made to the system before the next series of tests on that aircraft.\nThose improvements included doubling the number of smoke generators at each of\nthe previous six locations and adding two at the inboard edge of each inboard\nflap.\n\nEach of these 16 smoke generators had bleed air from the jet engines\n\nducted to their inlets to further improve their performance. This installa-\n\nAn example of the flow visualization produced by this arrangement is demonstrated in figure 3.\ntion has produced excellent results.\n\nA second type of flow visualization has been used which also performed\n\nadequately for either flow-field observation or for marking the wake to facilitate probing.\n\nThe wake in this case was marked with an inert powder blown into\n\nthe airstream at any location of interest.\nground diatomaceous earth.\n\nThe inert powder used is finely\n\nFigure 4 is a diagram of a typical installation.\n\nThe powder is contained in tanks located in the aircraft fuselage with tubing\nextending along or inside the wing to the desired location. When visualization is desired, the tanks are pressurized using bottled gas that expels the\npowder out the tubes.\n\nThis system is easy to install and is aerodynamically\n\nclean, but the visualization period is relatively short.\n\nThe powder is bulky\n\nand, for the size of tanks and delivery lines used, it is expelled in about 90\nsee.\n\nThe tanks must then be refilled (which can be done in flight) before\n\nadditional visualization can be accomplished.\n\nThis type of flow visualization\n\nwas used on a DC-3 and a DC-10 in programs at Flight Research Center (ref. 5)\nand on the Augmentor Wing Jet STOL Research Aircraft at Ames (ref. 9).\n\nFigure\n\n5 shows the installation of an inert powder system on a DC-10 and figure 6\nshows the visualization of the wake of a DC- 3 using a similar system.\nFlight Measurements\nFlight tests to determine the relative strengths of vortex systems have\nbeen made by penetrating the wake to measure either the upset imparted to a\nprobe aircraft or the flow velocities within the wake.\neither technique requires flow visualization.\n\nAs noted previously,\n\nIn addition, both techniques\n\nrequire that the distance between the probe and wake-generating aircraft b e\n\n197\n\nknown, and t h a t l i m i t s be placed on t h i s d i s t a n c e t o e n s u r e t h a t s t r u c t u r a l\nl i m i t l o a d s are n o t exceeded.\nThree d i f f e r e n t methods have been used i n wake t u r b u l e n c e f l i g h t programs\nconducted by NASA t o measure t h e d i s t a n c e between t h e probe a i r c r a f t and t h e\na i r c r a f t t h a t generated t h e wake.\n\nThe f i r s t technique r e q u i r e s t h e use of two\n\nBy t r a c k i n g each a i r c r a f t t o e s t a b l i s h i t s p o s i t i o n i n space\n\nground r a d a r s .\n\nas a f u n c t i o n of t i m e , t h e d i s t a n c e between t h e two a i r c r a f t can b e determined.\nThis technique has been used i n NASA programs a t Edwards and Vandenberg A i r\nForce Bases.\n\nThe i n f o r m a t i o n i s communicated t o t h e probe a i r c r a f t p i l o t t o\n\na i d him i n c o n t r o l l i n g t h e experiment.\n\nThe second technique used when r a d a r\n\nf a c i l i t i e s were n o t a v a i l a b l e i s t o conduct t h e f l i g h t t e s t w h i l e f l y i n g a\nr a d i a l toward (or away from) a d i s t a n t Distance Measuring Equipment (DME)\nground s t a t i o n .\n\nThe probe a i r c r a f t p i l o t c a l l s o u t h i s d i s t a n c e t o t h a t sta-\n\nt i o n as he encounters t h e wake.\n\nA t t h a t t i m e , t h e p i l o t of t h e wake-\n\ng e n e r a t i n g a i r c r a f t c a l l s out h i s d i s t a n c e t o t h e s t a t i o n and t h e d i f f e r e n c e\ni n t h e two i s t h e s e p a r a t i o n between t h e a i r c r a f t .\n\nBoth of t h e s e techniques\n\nare inconvenient i n t h a t e x t e n s i v e ground f a c i l i t i e s a r e r e q u i r e d o r a communic a t i o n l i n k i s r e q u i r e d t h a t can, a t t i m e s , be confusing.\nTo overcome t h e s e d i f f i c u l t i e s , a t h i r d system was developed:\na i r b o r n e DME\'s w a s modified (by S i e r r a Research Corp.,\n\na p a i r of\n\nBuffalo, New York) s o\n\nt h a t one of t h e p a i r performed as a beacon and t h e o t h e r as an i n t e r r o g a t o r of\nt h i o n s r e s u l t i n g from t h e aerodynamic c h a r a c t e r i s t i c s of t h e probe a i r c r a f t .\nThe s e p a r a t i o n d i s t a n c e between t h e two a i r c r a f t and t h e encounter a l t i t u d e\n\nare a l s o r e q u i r e d t o c o r r e l a t e the d a t a .\nUpset measurements are t a k e n w i t h t h e f l i g h t p a t h of t h e probing a i r c r a f t\nnominally p a r a l l e l t o t h e wake axes (henee t h e name " p a r a l l e l probes" ) ( f i g .\n\n7).\n\nWhen p r o b i n g t h e wake of an u n f a m i l i a r c o n f i g u r a t i o n , t h e i n i t i a l encoun-\n\nt e r w i t h t h e wake i s made a t a s e p a r a t i o n d i s t a n c e l a r g e enough t o ensure t h a t\nt h e r e s u l t i n g f o r c e s w i l l n o t s e v e r e l y l o a d t h e s t r u c t u r e of t h e a i r c r a f t .\nT h i s i s an a d d i t i o n a l p r e c a u t i o n t o t h e s a f e o p e r a t i o n procedures p r e v i o u s l y\n\n199\n\ndescribed.\n\nA s the probe aircraft encounters the wake, the pilot performs his\n\ntask in one of two modes.\n\nHe can hold the controls fixed, allowing the upset\n\nto remove the aircraft from the wake, or he can attempt to hold the probe air-\n\nplane as nearly centered in a vortex as possible.\n\nEither action produces the\n\ndesired data, but the pilot may obtain more subjective information by using a\ncombination of the two.\n\nThis procedure is repeated several times at each sepa-\n\nration distance to increase the probability that the maximum possible induced\naccelerations of the probe aircraft were achieved.\nThe instrumentation used to obtain the data required to derive the induced\nmoments is, generally, that which would be typical for a study of handling\nqualities.\n\nThe three-dimensional aircraft dynamics are measured by angular\n\naccelerometers, rate and attitude gyros, and linear accelerometers.\n\nThe deflec-\n\ntions of the control surfaces are measured as are airspeed, altitude, angles\nof attack and sideslip, and separation distance.\n\nTypical roll axis data are\n\nshown in figure 8.\nFigure 9 is a block diagram of the data-reduction process.\n\nDuring an\n\nencounter, the velocity gradients in the flow field to which the probe aircraft\nA s a result, the angles of attack and\n\nis being subjected can be quite high.\n\nsideslip measured on the nose boom or at any single point on the aircraft cannot be considered representative of the effective flow-field angularity to\nwhich the whole airframe is subjected.\n\nConsequently, it is necessary to com-\n\npute an effective angle of attack and sideslip from the measured linear accelerations and angular rates.\n\nThe angular accelerations that would have been\n\ncaused in still air by the aerodynamic forces and control inputs are computed.\nWhen subtracted from the measured angular accelerations, the accelerations\ninduced by the vortex flow are derived.\n\nComputed aerodynamic and control\n\naccelerations and net acceleration due to the vortex flow are shown in figure\n\n10 for the same encounters shown in figure 8.\n\nThe measured roll acceleration\n\nis repeated.\nA roll acceleration parameter, convenient for comparative purposes, was\n\ndeveloped to relate the vortex-induced roll acceleration9 \xe2\x80\x98vortex\xe2\x80\x99 to the maximum roll acceleration capability of the aircraft using maximum aileron deflection 5 8 %ax\nmoment was.\n\n.\n\nThe ratio Pvortex\xe2\x80\x9d 6%ax\n\nindicates how severe the induced\n\nThe physical meaning of this parameter is that, when a value of\n200\n\nunity is reached, the pilot could exactly counter the vortex-induced roll\nacceleration with maximum deflection of the ailerons.\n\nSimilar parameters can\n\nbe determined for accelerations in the pitch and yaw axes.\n\nFor each encounter\n\nbehind a given configuration, the peak values of this roll-acceleration parameter are plotted against the separation distance at which they occurred (fig.\n\n1)\n1. Assuming that the highest values at each distance represent the maximum\nupset expected at that location, an upper bound to the data is constructed.\nThe separation distance defined by the intersection of that boundary and the\nline for a unity roll acceleration parameter is used in comparisons with\nvalues obtained in a similar manner from data obtained during tests of other\naircraft or configurations.\n\nAn example of how these comparisons are made is\n\nshown in figure 12, where the separation distance for unity roll acceleration\nparameter is plotted against the gross weight of the wake-generating aircraft\nfor several sizes of probe aircraft.\n\nThis figure demonstrates that the upset\n\nreceived by a given probe aircraft is decreased as the gross weight of the\nwake-generating aircraft is decreased.\n\nSimilar comparisons can be made for the\n\nflight-test results of wake-vortex alleviation methods.\nVeZocity prof<Ze measurements\n\n- The need\n\nfor more definitive information\n\non the vortex-wake phenomenon led to the development of another flight-test\ntechnique.\n\nUse of this technique results in measurements of the velocity pro-\n\nfiles in the wake, which will allow comparisons of the wake structure with\ntheories (ref. 1)\n0.\n\nThis can be especially useful in the wake alleviation\n\nwork by more clearly defining the changes in the wake structure caused by an\nalleviation technique.\nThese measurements require a completely different technique in procedure,\ninstrumentation, and data reduction.\n\nThe flow velocities must be measured by\n\nsensors with higher frequency response characteristics than normally used in\nhandling qualities research, and the aircraft motion must be measured for use\nin correcting the velocity data.\n\nThe separation distance is again required to\n\ncorrelate the data.\nThe flight-test procedure used to obtain velocity profile data is considerably different from that used during upset measurements (fig. 1 3 ) .\n\nThe\n\nflight path of the probing aircraft is across the wake to effectively traverse\nthe flow field with the velocity-measuring probe.\n\n20 1\n\nIdeally, the flight path\n\nshould be in a plane perpendicular to the wake axes to minimize the motion\nexcursions for which corrections must be made.\n\nThe limited capability of the\n\nflow visualization systems to mark the wake results in an inability to see the\nwake well enough from right angles to enable the pilot of the probe aircraft\nA s the crossing angle is reduced,\n\nto penetrate the vortex cores consistently.\n\nthe apparent density of the flow visualization medium is increased, thereby\nallowing consistent core penetrations.\n\nLocal atmospheric conditions and the\n\nangle of the sun relative to the two aircraft affect visualization as well.\nExperience has shown that crossing angles of from 250 to 45O are adequate for\nthis task, These lower crossing angles have the additional advantage of allowing a shorter time between crossihgs because a turn of only 50\xc2\xb0 to 90\xc2\xb0 is\nrequired to traverse back across the wake.\n\nThis saving of time between wake\n\ncrossings allows more data to be obtained in the amount of time allowed by the\nflow visualization system.\nAs with the upset measurement procedures, wake encounters are begun at\nseparation distances large enough to assure safe operations.\n\nA zigzag flight\n\npath is flown while the separation distance is continually reduced until a\npredetermined minimum separation distance is reached.\n\nThis minimum separation\n\ndistance is set for safety reasons and, as for upset measurements, is based on\ncomputed loads, extrapolated load measurements, or probe pilot decisions.\nOnce the minimum separation distance is reached, a 360\xc2\xb0 turn is initiated which\nsets the probe aircraft up for additional data at a larger separation distance.\nThis procedure is repeated several times until adequate data are acquired for\nthat wake-generating aircraft configuration.\nThe frequency content of the velocity data being recorded as the probe\naircraft traverses the wake is considerably higher than that normally seen in\naircraft dynamics work.\n\n/\n\nAssuming reasonable values for the probe aircraft\n\nt,\nvelocity and the vortex core size of 120 m/sec (400 ft/sec) and 3 m (10 f )\nrespectively, the apparent frequency of the approximately sinusoidal input\nrecorded as the vortex core is penetrated is 40 Hz.\n\nThis frequency is higher\n\nthan conventional research aircraft flow angularity sensors are able to measure reliably.\n\nThree types of sensors have been used to this purpose.\n\nAngle\n\nof attack and sideslip vanes (fig. 1 4 ) have been used by Langley Research\n\nA five-hole pitot tube (fig. 1 5 )\nhas been used at Flight Research Center on a T-37 probe aircraft. At Ames\n\n)\nCenter on a T-33 research aircraft (ref. 8.\n\n20 2\n\nResearch Center, a three-component, hot-wire anemometer (fig. 16) has been\nused on a Learjet probe aircraft (ref. 21).\n\nThe instrumentation system and\n\ndata-reduction technique described below is that for the hot-wire anemometry\nsensors and is typical of the data-reduction technique required for the others\nas well.\n\nIn addition to the high-frequency velocity measurements, information\n\non the probe aircraft dynamics is required.\n\nThese are measured by angular and\n\nlinear accelerometers and angular rate and attitude gyros.\n\nThe range between\n\nthe aircraft whose wake is being studied and the probe aircraft is measured\ndirectly by a pair of modified DME\'s as in the upset measurements.\nThe data-reduction process is indicated by the flow chart in figure 17.\nThe airborne-recorded analog data are digitized at the rate of 1000 samples/sec.\n(This rate is necessary because of the high-frequency content of the data.)\nThe wake velocities are first computed from the hot-wire data in an axis system\naligned with the body axes of the aircraft, and corrections are made for the\nvertical velocity and pitch rate of the aircraft.\n\nCorrections are a l s o made\n\nfor the apparent vertical and lateral velocities that result from the effective\nangles of attack and sideslip generated as the aircraft reacts to the induced\nforces. The three components of velocity are then resolved into an axis system aligned with the axis of the vortex using the relative heading for each\npass across the wake.\n\nThe flight path of the probe is computed from the ini-\n\ntial airspeed and the measured vertical accelerations and pitching excursions.\n\nIn this way, a velocity distribution along a known path in space is determined.\nThe velocity profile measured at a separation distance of 2 9 nautical\n.\nmiles behind a B-747 in its normal landing configuration is presented in figure 1 . All three components of velocity are shown, the top one being the\n8\nvertical velocity component defined as positive upward, the middle trace is\nthe lateral component defined as positive to the left, and the bottom trace is\nthe axial velocity component defined positive toward the aircraft that generated the wake.\n\nComparing these data with that of the next figure demonstrates\n\nthe ability of this system to show the effects of vortex-wake alleviation in\nthe measured flow field.\n\nA comparison of figures 18 and 19 indicates the\n\nreduction in the three components of velocity in the wake of the B-747 at 2.2\nnautical miles with the outboard flaps retracted.\n\n20 3\n\nP\n\nAnother purpose for which these data can be used is to understand, or at\nleast dacument., the changes in the vortex structure as it ages.\n\nSince no two\n\ntraverses of the wake are the same, these comparisons are best made by fitting\ndata of various ages with a mathematical model and observing the changes in\nthe model parameters as the vortex ages.\n\nFor the initial attempts at modeling\n\nthese data, a pair of Lamb vortices was selected.\n\nThis representation is con-\n\nMatching the computed velocities\n\nvenient because the formulation is analytic.\n\nto the measured data along the actual flight path was accompl3shed using the\nlocations of the two vortices, the circulation of each vortex, and their core\nsize as parameters.\n\nThe match was done in a least squares sense on the verti-\n\ncal and lateral velocity components simultaneously.\n\nA result of that matching\n\nprocedure is seen in figure 20, where the velocities computed from the model\nas they would have been measured along the flight path are compared with the\nmeasured data.\n\nIf the wake can be adequately represented by a single pair of\n\nvortices, this procedure results in a satisfactory match.\n\nModeling the wake\n\nat various separation distances and comparing the results can show the decay\n\n1.\nof the velocities in the vortex (fig. 2 )\n\nThe reduction of maximum velocity\n\nis seen as the separation distance increases.\nAn application of this mathematical modeling technique to the wake alleviation work is shown in figure 22, where a model of the tangential velocity\nprofile of the B-747 wake at separation distances up to 3 1 nautical miles in\n.\nnormal landing configuration is compared with that for the same airplane\'s\nwake at distances as close as 1 0 nautical mile with the outboard flap segment\n.\nretracted. Even though the profiles for the latter case are at closer separation distances, the peak tangential velocity is reduced and the effective core\nsize increased.\nREFERENCES\n\n1 Corsiglia, V R ; ROSSOW, V. The flight data in this figure has been corrected to the\nsame value of U/Uf\n\n(where Uf\n\nis the velocity of the following aircraft) as\n\nthe 0.07 scale tests.\nA flight rolling moment coefficient of the 0.058 is obtained for the basic\n\nconfiguration at a separation distance of 0.70 km using the straight line extrapolation shown. This extrapolation is based on flight test results presented by\nMarvin R. Barber, et al, in this report. There is reasonable agreement between\nthe "no splines" data based on this extrapolation. This indicates that the test\ntechniques employed in the ground and the flight experiments were valid for this\nconfiguration.\nThe attenuated rolling-moment coefficients (splines on) presented in figure 14 are for the wing-tip mounted spline device with a diameter equal to\n\n55 percent of the mean geometric wing chord. It was possible during the flight\ntest to probe as close as 0.60 km behind the vortex-generating aircraft allowing\na direct comparison between flight and model test results.\nThe tip-mounted spline reduced the vortex-induced rolling moment in flight\napproximately 72 percent at a separation distance of 0.70 km while the model\nresults indicate a vortex roll reduction of approximately 27 percent at this\nsame separation distance.\n\nThese data indicate that a greater vortex attenuating\n\neffect is derived from the spline device in flight than in the ground facility.\nAlthough the reason for this discrepancy has not yet been determined it might be\nconjectured then that Reynolds number may have a large affect on the flow field\ndeveloped by the spline device reducing its vortex attenuating effect at model\nscale. The model visual data indicate that the well-organized vortex core is\nnearly eliminated while the circulation outside of the core remains and produces\nthe rolling moment measured by the following model.\n\nThe lower velocities asso-\n\nciated with the circulation would be affected by model scale.\n\n288\n\n,\n\nAPPLICATIONS STUDY\n\nDuring t h e s e f l i g h t tests, t h e performance of t h e generating a i r c r a f t\nwith s p l i n e s a t t a c h e d w a s evaluated and found t o b e a c c e p t a b l e ( r e f . 3 ) .\nHowever, because t h i s generating a i r c r a f t w a s n o t a r e p r e s e n t a t i v e commercial\na i r c r a f t , a preliminary study w a s conducted f o r NASA by the Boeing Go. t o\ne v a l u a t e t h e performance of t h e a i r c r a f t shown i n f i g u r e 15.\n\nResults are\n\npresented i n r e f e r e n c e 5.\nI n summary, i t w a s concluded t h a t f o r s p l i n e s deployed only on approach,\nt h e concept appears f e a s i b l e from t h e s t a n d p o i n t of s t r u c t u r e s , s t a b i l i t y and\nc o n t r o l , performance, and operations.\n\nThe l a r g e s t s p l i n e e f f e c t s are decreases\n\ni n landing climb g r a d i e n t and climb r a t e , and an i n c r e a s e i n approach n o i s e .\nEven with t h e s e p e n a l t i e s , t h e a i r c r a f t would meet c e r t i f i c a t i o n requirements.\nE f f e c t s on o t h e r parameters such as t a k e o f f , c r u i s e , range, approach speed,\nand s o f o r t h , were i n d i c a t e d t o be i n s i g n i f i c a n t .\n\nCONCLUDING REMARKS\n\nThe r e s u l t s obtained during t h i s i n v e s t i g a t i o n i n d i c a t e that- -\n\n(1) The s p l i n e device i s e f f e c t i v e i n reducing t h e s t r e n g t h of t h e\nl i f t - i n d u c e d vortex.\n(2)\n\nA comparison of t h e vortex- induced r o l l i n g moment obtained i n t h e\n\nLangley Vortex Research F a c i l i t y with f u l l - s c a l e f l i g h t r e s u l t s\ni n d i c a t e good agreement f o r t h e unattenuated a i r c r a f t c o n f i g u r a t i o n .\nThis implies t h a t t h e test techniques employed on t h e ground and\ni n f l i g h t experiments w e r e v a l i d .\n\n289\n\n(3)\n\nA comparison of d a t a a t 0.70 km i n d i c a t e s t h a t t h e v o r t e x\na t t e n u a t i o n achieved i n f l i g h t w a s g r e a t e r than w a s o b t a i n e d i n\nt h e ground f a c i l i t y , i n d i c a t i n g that t h e ground f a c i l i t y w a s\nc o n s e r v a t i v e w i t h t h i s device.\n\n(4)\n\nA p r e l i m i n a r y s t u d y i n d i c a t e d t h a t t h e i n s t a l l a t i o n of s p l i n e s on\nc u r r e n t commercial j e t a i r c r a f t would b e f e a s i b l e .\n\nSignificant\n\np e n a l t i e s would r e s u l t i n t h e l a n d i n g phase climb c a p a b i l i t y and\napproach n o i s e .\n\nEven w i t h t h e p e n a l t i e s , however, t h i s a i r c r a f t\n\nwould m e e t c u r r e n t c e r t i f i c a t i o n requirements.\n\nREFERENCES\n\n1.\n\nP a t t e r s o n , J. ATTENUATION FLIGHT EXPERIMENTS\nMarvin R . Barber, Earl C . Hastings , Jr .,*\nRobert A . Champine, * and Joseph J . TymczyszynP\nDryden Flight Research Center\nSUMMARY\n\nFlight tests evaluating the effects of altered span loading, turbulence ingestion,\ncombinations of mass and turbulence ingestion, and combinations of altered span\nloading and turbulence ingestion on trailed wake vortex attenuation were conducted\nin several NASA flight test programs. Span loadings were altered in flight by\nvarying the deflections of the inboard and outboard flaps on a B-747 aircraft.\nTurbulence ingestion was achieved in flight by mounting splines on a C-54G aircraft.\nMass and turbulence ingestion was achieved in flight by varying the thrust. on the\nB-747 aircraft. Combinations of altered span loading and turbulence ingestion were \'\nachieved in flight by installing a spoiler on a CV-990 aircraft and by deflecting the\nexisting spoilers on a B-747 aircraft.\nThe characteristics of the attenuated and unattenuated vortexes were determined\nby probing them with smaller aircraft. Acceptable separation distances for encounters with the attenuated and unattenuated vortexes are presented.\nINTRODUCTION\nFlight tests evaluating the effects of altered span loading, turbulence ingestion,\ncombinations of mass and turbulence ingestion, and combinations of altered span\nloading and turbulence ingestion on trailed wake vortex attenuation were conducted\n*Langley Research Center.\n\'Federal Aviation Administration, Western Region.\n369\n\nin several NASA flight test programs. The programs were initiated .because ground\nfacility (wind tunnel and water tank) measurements indicated that certain configuration changes offered potential for vortex attenuation, with a resultant increase in\nsafety and airport capacity.\nTable 1 summarizes the vortex attenuation flight experiments. The table also\nlists the aircraft involved in each experiment and gives some idea of the magnitude\nand timing of the programs.\nSpan loadings were altered in flight by varying the deflections of the inboard\nand outboard flaps on a Boeing B-747 aircraft. Figure 1 (a) shows the B-747 airplane\' flying with a conventional or 30/ 30 landing flap configuration (i .e . , inboard\nflap deflected 30\xc2\xb0 and outboard flap deflected 30O). Figure l(b) shows the airplane\nflying with the 3 0 / 1 flap configuration (inboard flap deflected 3 0 \xc2\xb0 , outboard flap\ndeflected lo). The following combinations of inboard/outboard flap deflections were\ntested:\n\n30/30, 30/20, 3 0 / 1 0 , 3 0 / 5 , 3 0 / 1 , and 5 / 3 0 . The minimum outboard flap\n\ndeflection was lo so that the leading-edge flaps could be kept extended (the leadingedge flap deflections are programed according to trailing-edge flap deflections)\n\n.\n\nVarious span loads were tested because of the attenuation potential forecast by the\nwind-tunnel tests reported in reference 1 . The results of the flight tests of span\nloading alterations are reported in references 2 and 3 .\nTurbulence was created and ingested into the vortexes in flight by mounting\nsplines on a McDonnell Douglas C-54G aircraft and by varying the thrust of the\nengines on the B-747 aircraft. Figure 2 shows the splines on the aircraft in flight,\nand figure 3 shows details of the splines. These tests were preceded by windtunnel tests which are reported in conjunction with the flight tests in reference 4 .\nThe inboard and outboard engines of the B-747 airplane are alined directly in\nfront of the outboard edges of the inboard and outboard flaps, respectively.\nFigure 1 illustrates the engine/flap alinement\n\n.\n\nThis alinement naturally caused\n\ncuriosity about the effects of changes in engine thrust on the wake vortexes of the\nB-747 airplane in the normal landing configuration and in attenuated configurations.\n\nTherefore, the effects of engine thrust were evaluated on numerous occasions\nthroughout the B-747 vortex attenuation flight tests.\nThe combined effects of altered span loading and turbulence ingestion were\ntested in flight by installing a fixed spoiler on the wingtip of a Convair CV-990 aircraft and by deflecting the existing spoilers on the B-747 airplane. Figures 4(a)\n\n37 0\n\nand 4(b) show the CV-990 wingtip spoiler from a distance and close up. Windtunnel tests of this configuration were conducted and reported in conjunction with\nflight tests in reference 5 .\nFigure 5 is a sketch of the B-747 spoiler/speed brakes, referred to herein as\nspoilers. The effects of various spoiler segment combinations on vortex attenuation\nwere tested thoroughly in the Langley V/STOL wind tunnel (ref. 6 ) . These results\nwere sufficiently promising to warrant flight test verification. Therefore, the\neffects of deflecting these spoilers on vortex attenuation are being evaluated in flight\ntests.\nAll the B-747 flight tests were conducted with the NASA B-747 space shuttle\ncarrier aircraft, and because the airplane was not always available, some of the\ntests could not be made before this writing. The scheduling problem also prevented\nmany of the quantitative data from being processed in time for this paper. This\n. paper therefore summarizes the qualitative assessment of the vortex attemation pro-\n\nvided by some of the B-747 configurations,\nSYMBOLS\n\nI zj (measured\n\nroll axis angular acceleration measured during vortex encounter\n\nlb16\n\nmaximum roll axis angular acceleration that can be generated by\n\na\n\nmax\n\nvortex-induced rolling-moment coefficient\n\n5V\nC\n\n6\n\n\xe2\x80\x988 \xe2\x80\x98max\na\n\nC\n\nailerons\n\n.maximum rolling-moment coefficient that can be generated by\nailerons\nmean aerodynamic chord\nTEST TECHNIQUES\n\nThe technique of vortex attenuation flight testing has been described in detail\nin the paper by Jacobsen and Barber earlier in this conference. However, a review\nof the techniques from the pilot\xe2\x80\x99s standpoint is believed to be appropriate.\n\n371\n\n*\n\nThe most significant test in a qualitative evaluation of the vortex hazard to a\ntrailing aircraft consists of probing the vortex as it would most likely be encountered\nin a real landing approach situation. To simulate this situation, the vortexes are\nprobed with as small a penetration angle as possible. This type of probe is referred\nto as a parallel probe (i.e. , the probing aircraft\'s flight path is approximately\nparallel to the vortex). The probe aircraft may enter the vortex wake from below,\nfrom above, or from either side. The probes result in pitch, roll, and yaw upsets\nthat are representative of those that would be experienced by trailing aircraft in\nreal situations. Qualitative assessments of these upsets enable the probe pilot to\nselect separation distances (or times) behind a generating aircraft that result in\nacceptable levels of vortex-induced hazard.\nThe only limitation on a simulation of real vortex encounter situations is test\naltitude. Statistically it is known that most accidents attributed to trailed wake\nvortexes occur on landing approach at relatively low altitudes. Unfortunately,\nflight test experience to date has not resulted in sufficient confidence in either\npredictive techniques o r the repeatability of flight test results to permit testing at\naltitudes less than those required for comfortable recovery from inverted flight. In\npractice, this means the tests must be conducted no lower than approximately\n1500 meters (5000 feet) above ground level.\nTherefore, the probe pilot is evaluating an upset hazard at high altitudes above\nground level and attempting to extrapolate his evaluation to low altitudes. This\nshortcoming has become more and more significant as the attenuated vortex configurations have enabled vortex encounters at closer separation distances, thereby\nincreasing the need to verify the acceptability of the configuration.\nIn the past, probe pilots have estimated minimum separation distances by\ndetermining the separation distance at which the vortex-induced upsets would cause\nthem to decide to execute a missed approach if they were performing an IFR approach.\nA s noted, these determinations were made during parallel probes made approximately\n1500 meters (5000 feet) above ground level.\n\nFigure 6 summarizes the data that were obtained from tests reported in references 7 to 10. The correlation of the data throughout the numerous test programs is\nrelatively good and illustrates that the pilots\' opinions obtained by using the previously discussed technique agree remarkably well with the minimum separation\ndistances that were established by using the roll control criterion discussed by\n\n37 2\n\nJacobsen and Barber. These data and the techniques for obtaining them were the\nbaseline from which the vortex attenuation flight tests to be discussed herein were\ninitiated. Experience gained in the vortex attenuation flight tests show some shortcomings in these data and indicate areas where present techniques a r e inadequate.\nTEST RESULTS\nAltered Span Loading\nSpan loadings were altered in flight by varying the deflections of .the inboard\nand outboard flaps on a B-747 aircraft as shown in figures l ( a ) and l(b) . Seventeen\nflights were flown by the B-747 airplane to complete these tests, and the Cessna\nT-37B and Learjet-23 (LR-23) aircraft were utilized as the probe aircraft. Both\nparallel and cross-track probes were performed during this test series. Most of the\ntests were preceded by the wind-tunnel tests reported in reference 1 , and were\npartially reported in references 2 and 3.\nThe results of these tests are summarized in figure 7 , which presents the pilots\'\nqualitative assessments of the alleviation provided by retracting the outboard flap\non the B-747 airplane. The landing-gear-up data show that a significant amount of\nattenuation was provided by retracting the outboard flap. These data agree with\nwind-tunnel data obtained from a model that did not have a landing gear, When the\nlanding gear on the airplane was extended, a significant amount of the attenuation\nwas lost.\nAn assessment of the correlation between the pilots\' qualitative separation\nrequirements and the requirements that would b e dictated by the roll control\ncriterion can be obtained by comparing figure 7 with figures 8(a) and 8 ( b ) . In\ngeneral, these data show that the quantitative and qualitative requirements are in\nagreement.\nDuring these tests it was also noted that engine thrust and aircraft sideslip had\na significant effect on the attenuation provided by the altered span loadings. These\neffects are illustrated in figures 9 and 1 0 .\nThe differences in the B-747 trailed vortex system with and without the outboard\nflap extended can be evaluated by reviewing the photographs in figures ll(a) to l l ( j )\nand figures 1 2 (a) to 1 2 (g) . A comparison of figure 1 (a) and figure 1 2 (a) indicates\n1\nthat with the outboard flap extended the dominant vortex is the vortex shed from\n\n373\n\n.\n\nthe outboard edge of the outboard flap. With the outboard flap retracted, the\ndominant vortex is that shed from the outboard edge of the inboard flap. In both\ncases, the wingtip vortex interacts with the dominant vortex. The effects of configuration on vortex life can also be compared. The unattenuated vortex persists for\nat least 45 seconds, when the cameraman ceased taking pictures. The attenuated\nvortex disappears completely in 30 seconds, After these tests, the vortex marking\nsystem was modified to include smokers at the inboard edge of the inboard flap to\ntry to determine why the landing gear reduced the attenuation. The photographs in\nfigures 13(a) and 13@) show that the landing gear diffuses a powerful vortex at\nthe inboard edge of the inboard flap, significantly reducing the attenuation provided\nby the 30/ 1 flap configuration. Though not available in the photographic record at\nthis time, visual observations of the inboard vortex showed that it intermingled with\n\nthe vortex off the outboard edge of the inboard flap when it was not diffused by\nthe landing gear.\nAlthough interesting from a research standpoint, the vortex attenuation afforded\nby the 30/1 flap configuration was disregarded for obvious operational reasons when\nthe degrading effects of the landing gear were discovered. It should be noted that\nthis configuration also imposed a center-of-gravity limitation on the B-747 airplane.\nA flight evaluation of the vortexes 4 nautical miles to 6 nautical miles behind the\nB-747 airplane in the 5/ 30 flap configuration was short and conclusive. Whereas\n\nthe 30/ 1flap configuration produced marked attenuation, the 5/30 vortex resulted in\nT-37B encounters that were even more violent than those caused by the conventional\n30/30 landing configuration at 6 nautical miles. One encounter at 6 nautical miles\n\nproduced a violent double snap roll which far exceeded the capability of the roll rate\ndata acquisition system and also caused an engine flameout. These tests showed the\ncorrect approach to vortex attenuation to be increasing the inboard span loading,\nTurbulence Ingestion\nThe ingestion of turbulence into the vortexes in flight was accomplished by\nmounting splines on the wingtips of a C-546 aircraft (figs. 2 and 3). The Piper\nCherokee (PA-28) airplane was used as the probe aircraft for these tests. The\nvortex attenuation results of these tests are shown in figure 14, which compares\nthe probe aircraft\'s roll accelerations in the attenuated and unattenuated vortexes.\nThe data show that the PA-28 airplane has insufficient roll control power to overcome\n374\n\nthe vortexes of the basic C-54G airplane at a separation distance of approximately\n4 nautical miles, and that maximum roll control power is never required to oppose\n\nthe vortex attenuated by splines. The data generally correlate with the pilots\' opinions of the attenuation, which w a s that roll control became insufficient approximately\n2 . 5 nautical miles behind the unattenuated C-54G airplane but that it was sufficient\nthroughout the entire range of separations tested for the attenuated configuration.\nThe unattenuated data represent the C-54G airplane in the clean configuration and\ntherefore are not directly comparable to the data presented in figure 6 , which are\nfor the normal landing configuration.\n\nThe effects of the splines on the performance, handling qualities, and noise of\nthe C-54G airplane were also measured. It was concluded that although the splines\nsignificantly reduced the rate of climb of the C-546 airplane, the airplane\'s fourengine performance was acceptable for this test program. (It should be noted that\nthe splines were not retractable, as they would be for any configuration seriously\nproposed for operation .) The splines caused no noticeable changes in the handling\nqualities of the C-54G airplane. Finally, the maximum overall sound pressure level\nof the C-54G airplane during landing approach with splines on was approximately\n4 decibels higher than with splines off.\nThe vortex attenuation potential of the splines should not be too easily discounted\nbecause of what may seem to be rather complex operational problems. An unpublished study concerning the feasibility of producing retractable splines concluded\n*\n\nthat the concept is practical.\nCombinations of Mass and Turbulence Ingestion\nFlight tests were conducted with the B- 747 airplane to evaluate the effects of\nengine thrust on vortex attenuation. In general, most of the B-747 testing has been\nconducted with thrust for level flight at altitudes of approximately 3000 meters\n(10,000 feet) , because a level flightpath makes it easier for the probe pilots to find\nand encounter the vortexes. However, considerable testing has been performed for\nall the attenuated and unattenuated configurations wherein the thrust was reduced\nfrom that required for level flight to that required for a -3O flightpath angle-and\nfurther to flight idle (approximately - 6 O flightpath angle, depending on spoiler,\nflap, and gear configuration). To date, a detailed comparison of the pilots\' qualitative assessments of the effects of engine thrust with quantitative data has not been\n375\n\ncompleted. In general however it appears that reducing the thrust from that\nrequired for level flight to flight idle adds approximately 2 nautical miles to the\nrequired separation distance. This generalization is true for both the 30/30 and\n30/1 flap configurations (fig. 9 ) .\n\nTests wherein the inboard and outboard engine thrust levels have been varied\nalternately have been conducted, but the data are not yet available. Tests to\ndetermine the effects of engine thrust on the attenuation provided by deflecting\nvarious spoiler segments are yet to be completed.\nCombinations of Span Loading Alteration and Turbulence Ingestion\nWind-tunnel tests made as early as 1969 indicated that the character of the\ntrailing vortex system could be changed significantly by adding a spoiler to the\nwing in the area of the vortex formation (ref. 5). Flight tests of a spoiler on the\nwingtip of a CV-990 aircraft (fig. 4) were conducted in 1970 as a result of these\nwind-tunnel tests. Unfortunately, at that time in-flight vortex marking systems\nwere not available, and therefore the tests were rather inconclusive.\nMore recently however, wind-tunnel tests have shown that extending various\ncombinations of the B-747 spoilers is effective in attenuating its vortexes (ref. 6 ) .\nFigure 5 shows that the four outboard spoiler panels on the B-747 airplane are in\nthe vicinity of the outboard flap where the dominant vortex is shed (fig. 11).\nTherefore, it is not surprising that extending these spoilers affects the resulting\nvortex system.\nThese tests were paced by wind-tunnel tests (ref. 6 ) . In flight, the spoilers\nwere deflected in the following combinations:\n\nI\n\nSpoiler panel\n\n37 6\n\nI\n\nThe deflection angles were chosen as a result of the flight crew\'s concern about the\nlevel of buffet induced by the spoilers and limitations of the control system on the\n7\nproduction B-747 airplane. The 3 O deflection for spoilers 3, 4, 9 , and 10 and the\n41\xc2\xb0 deflection for spoilers 1, 2 , 11, and 1 2 were used because of the crew\'s concern\n\nabout the safety of accepting a higher level of buffet. The 2 5 O limit for the deflection of spoilers 1, 2, 11, and 1 2 was established because it caused the highest buffet\nlevel that the flight crew felt passengers would tolerate. The 4 5 O deflections for the\nspoiler combinations (1, 4 , 9 , 12) and (2, 3, 10, 11) were limited primarily by the\ncontrol system. It is interesting to note that the flight crew felt that the buffet level\nwith a spoiler deflection of 45O was excessive for spoiler combination (2, 3, 10, 11),\nbut acceptable for the spoiler combination (1, 4 , 9 , 12) ,\nFigure 15 summarizes the pilots\' qualitative separation requirements from the\nspoiler flight tests. The data illustrate that with spoilers 1, 2, 11, and 1 2 deflected\n41\xc2\xb0, significantly more attenuation is provided than with any other configuration.\n\nIn fact, it would appear from these data that the 41\xc2\xb0 deflection of spoilers 1, 2 , 11,\nand 12 could be proposed as an operational configuration that would allow light\naircraft to be spaced as close as 3 nautical miles behind heavy aircraft. Therefore ,\na series of tests was developed to investigate the operational feasibility of using\nthis configuration. This investigation was to include actual landings of the B-747\nairplane with the spoilers extended and probes of its vortex at landing flare altitudes\nby the T-37B airplane.\nLanding the B-747 airplane with the spoilers extended was accomplished in a\nrelatively straightforward manner, and the pilots indicated that the spoilers did not\nsignificantly detract from the airplane\'s landing performance.\nThe proposed low altitude probes with the T-37B airplane required a reevaluation of the criteria on which probing was based at altitude. A s discussed in\nTEST TECHNIQUES , the probe pilots used as a criterion the level of upsets which\nwould force them to abandon an approach either on instruments or after breaking\nout at the bottom of an overcast. Among other factors, a bank angle limit of approximately 30\xc2\xb0 for the T-37B airplane at altitude was considered as a baseline (with\nlower limits for aircraft with larger wingspans). The adequacy of this partial\ncriterion was questioned , however, when actual landings were proposed; lower\ncontrol power , proximity to stalls or spins at low altitude, and the thrust required\n\n377 \'\n\nto overcome the downwash of the generating airplane became additional items of\nconcern.\nIntentional probes of the downwash area between the B-747 vortexes were made\nwith the B-747 airplane with spoilers 1, 2, 11, and 12 deflected. The T-37B airplane probed this area to less than 2 nautical miles and found only light to moderate\nturbulence with an incremental downwash of approximately 150 meters per minute\n(500 feet per minute). The problem of adequate climb performance at low speeds in\n\nthe landing configuration had to be considered, even when roll control power was\nadequate to overcome the vortex-induced roll. An additional unknown was the\nvariation of vortex strength and life as a function of altitude and ground effect.\nTests using ground-based sensors were conducted to evaluate the normal and\nattenuated vortex characteristics, but the results are not yet available.\nAnother question pertinent to the problem of separation distance is the effectiveness of attenuating devices for following aircraft that have considerably larger\nwingspans than the T-37B and LR-23 airplanes. Wind-tunnel and water tank tests\non a McDonnell Douglas DC-9 scale model indicate a definite reduction in induced\nroll , but not as large a reduction as models of smaller wingspan experienced. This\neffect is due to the better fit of an airplane with a larger wingspan in the larger\nattenuated vortex.\nAll T-37B probe pilots agree that the attenuated vortex is much larger in\n\ndiameter, is less well defined, and has lower tangential velocities than the usual,\nwell-formed vortex tube. A DC-9 airplane is being instrumented and is to be test\nflown to determine the degree of effectiveness of vortex attenuation on an airplane\nwith a significantly larger wingspan than the T-37B and LR-23 aircraft.\nA comparison of the photographs presented in figures 16(a) and 16(b) with\n\nthose shown in figures 17(a) and 17(b) will provide the reader with an illustration\n\nof the differences in the formation of the B-747 vortex systems with and without\nspoilers 1, 2 , 11, and 12 extended.\nCONCLUDING REMARKS\n\nFlight tests made to evaluate the effects of altered span loading, turbulence\ningestion , combinations of mass and turbulence ingestion, and combinations of\naltered span loading and turbulence ingestion on trailed wake vortex attenuation\n37 8\n\nshowed aerodynamic attenuation to be possible and probably operationally practical.\nThis conclusion is based on the fact that three of the methods tried provided significant levels of wake vortex attenuation.\nREFERENCES\n\n.\n\n.\n\n1.
